13 The weather was very hot ; but Nur Mahomed thought that the sooner his precious letter was delivered the better ; so that , by dint of riding most of each night and resting only in the hottest part of the day , he found himself , by noon on the third day , approaching the town which was his final destination .
14 Not a soul was to be seen anywhere ; and Nur Mahomed , stiff , dry , thirsty , and tired , looked longingly over the wall into the gardens , and marked the fountains , the green grass , the shady apricot orchards , and giant mulberry trees , and wished he were there .
15 At length he reached the castle gates , and was at once admitted , as he was in the uniform of the king 's bodyguard .
16 The governor was resting , the soldier said , and could not see him until the evening .
17 So Nur Mahomed handed over his horse to an attendant , and wandered down into the lovely gardens he had seen from the road , and sat down in the shade to rest himself .
18 He flung himself on his back and watched the birds twittering and chattering in the trees above him .
19 Through the branches he could see great patches of sky where the kites wheeled and circled incessantly , with shrill whistling cried .
20 Bees buzzed over the flowers with a soothing sound , and in a few minutes Nur Mahomed was fast asleep .
21 Every day , through the heat of the afternoon , the governor , and his wife also , used to lie down for two or three hours in their own rooms , and so , for the matter of that , did most people in the XXXXX .	palace		grass|house|journey|man|palace|presence|road|soldier|wall|weather

1 ` Make your preparations at once , ' added he , ` and be ready to start in the morning .
2 I myself will deliver you the papers at the last moment . '
3 Now this province was four or five days ' journey from the palace , and the governor of it was the most faithful servant the king had .
4 He could be silent as the grave , and prided himself on his obedience .
5 Whilst he was an old and tried servant of the king 's , his wife had been almost a mother to the young princess ever since the queen had died some years before .
6 It happened that , a little before this time , the princess had been sent away for her health to another remote province ; and whilst she was there her old friend , the governor 's wife , had begged her to come and stay with them as soon as she could .
7 The princess accepted gladly , and was actually staying in the governor 's house at the very time when the king made up his mind to send Nur Mahomed there with the mysterious despatch .
8 According to orders Nur Mahomed presented himself early the next morning at the king 's private apartments .
9 His best horse was saddled , food placed in is saddle-bag , and with some money tied up in his waist-band , he was ready to start .
10 The king handed over to him a sealed packet , desiring him to give it himself only into the hands of the governor , and to no one else .
11 Nur Mahomed hid it carefully in his turban , swung himself into the saddle , and five minutes later rode out of the city gates , and set out on his long journey .
12 The weather was very hot ; but Nur Mahomed thought that the sooner his precious letter was delivered the better ; so that , by dint of riding most of each night and resting only in the hottest part of the day , he found himself , by noon on the third day , approaching the town which was his final destination .
13 Not a soul was to be seen anywhere ; and Nur Mahomed , stiff , dry , thirsty , and tired , looked longingly over the wall into the gardens , and marked the fountains , the green grass , the shady apricot orchards , and giant mulberry trees , and wished he were there .
14 At length he reached the castle gates , and was at once admitted , as he was in the uniform of the king 's bodyguard .
15 The governor was resting , the soldier said , and could not see him until the evening .
16 So Nur Mahomed handed over his horse to an attendant , and wandered down into the lovely gardens he had seen from the road , and sat down in the shade to rest himself .
17 He flung himself on his back and watched the birds twittering and chattering in the trees above him .
18 Through the branches he could see great patches of sky where the kites wheeled and circled incessantly , with shrill whistling cried .
19 Bees buzzed over the flowers with a soothing sound , and in a few minutes Nur Mahomed was fast asleep .
20 Every day , through the heat of the afternoon , the governor , and his wife also , used to lie down for two or three hours in their own rooms , and so , for the matter of that , did most people in the palace .
21 But the XXXXX , like many other girls , was restless , and preferred to wander about the garden , rather than rest on a pile of soft cushions .	princess		back|day|destination|grave|house|palace|princess|queen|town|weather

18 This very afternoon the princess had left all her women asleep , and , after trying in vain to amuse herself indoors , she had slipped out into the great garden , and rambled about in all her favourite nooks and corners , feeling quite safe as there was not a creature to be seen .
19 Suddenly , on turning a corner , she stopped in surprise , for before her lay a man fast asleep !
20 In her hurry she had almost tripped over him .

3 Nur Mahomed hid it carefully in his turban , swung himself into the saddle , and five minutes later rode out of the city gates , and set out on his long journey .
4 The weather was very hot ; but Nur Mahomed thought that the sooner his precious letter was delivered the better ; so that , by dint of riding most of each night and resting only in the hottest part of the day , he found himself , by noon on the third day , approaching the town which was his final destination .
5 Not a soul was to be seen anywhere ; and Nur Mahomed , stiff , dry , thirsty , and tired , looked longingly over the wall into the gardens , and marked the fountains , the green grass , the shady apricot orchards , and giant mulberry trees , and wished he were there .
6 At length he reached the castle gates , and was at once admitted , as he was in the uniform of the king 's bodyguard .
7 The governor was resting , the soldier said , and could not see him until the evening .
8 So Nur Mahomed handed over his horse to an attendant , and wandered down into the lovely gardens he had seen from the road , and sat down in the shade to rest himself .
9 He flung himself on his back and watched the birds twittering and chattering in the trees above him .
10 Through the branches he could see great patches of sky where the kites wheeled and circled incessantly , with shrill whistling cried .
11 Bees buzzed over the flowers with a soothing sound , and in a few minutes Nur Mahomed was fast asleep .
12 Every day , through the heat of the afternoon , the governor , and his wife also , used to lie down for two or three hours in their own rooms , and so , for the matter of that , did most people in the palace .
13 But the princess , like many other girls , was restless , and preferred to wander about the garden , rather than rest on a pile of soft cushions .
14 What a torment her stout old attendants and servants sometime thought her when she insisted on staying awake , and making them chatter or do something , when they could hardly keep their eyes open !
15 Sometimes , however , the princess would pretend to go to sleep , and then , after all her women had gladly followed her example , she would get up and go out by herself , her veil hanging loosely about her .
16 If she was discovered her old hostess scolded her severely ; but the princess only laughed , and did the same thing next time .
17 This very afternoon the princess had left all her women asleep , and , after trying in vain to amuse herself indoors , she had slipped out into the great garden , and rambled about in all her favourite nooks and corners , feeling quite safe as there was not a creature to be seen .
18 Suddenly , on turning a corner , she stopped in surprise , for before her lay a man fast asleep !
19 In her hurry she had almost tripped over him .
20 But there he was , a young man , tanned and dusty with travel , in the uniform of an officer of the king 's guard .
21 One of the few faults of this lovely XXXXX was a devouring curiosity , and she lived such an idle life that she had plenty of time to be curious .	princess		example|eyes|food|nooks|princess|shade|soldier|surprise|travel|trees

7 What a torment her stout old attendants and servants sometime thought her when she insisted on staying awake , and making them chatter or do something , when they could hardly keep their eyes open !
8 Sometimes , however , the princess would pretend to go to sleep , and then , after all her women had gladly followed her example , she would get up and go out by herself , her veil hanging loosely about her .
9 If she was discovered her old hostess scolded her severely ; but the princess only laughed , and did the same thing next time .
10 This very afternoon the princess had left all her women asleep , and , after trying in vain to amuse herself indoors , she had slipped out into the great garden , and rambled about in all her favourite nooks and corners , feeling quite safe as there was not a creature to be seen .
11 Suddenly , on turning a corner , she stopped in surprise , for before her lay a man fast asleep !
12 In her hurry she had almost tripped over him .
13 But there he was , a young man , tanned and dusty with travel , in the uniform of an officer of the king 's guard .
14 One of the few faults of this lovely princess was a devouring curiosity , and she lived such an idle life that she had plenty of time to be curious .
15 Out of one of the folds of this young man 's turban there peeped the corner of a letter !
16 She wondered what the letter was -- whom it was for !
17 She drew her veil a little closer , and stole across on tip-toe and caught hold of the corner of the letter .
18 Then she pulled it a little , and just a little more !
19 A great big seal came into view , which she saw to be her father 's , and at the sight of it she paused for a minute half ashamed of what she was doing .
20 But the pleasure of taking a letter which was not meant for her was more than she could resist , and in another moment it was in her hand .
21 All at once she remembered that it would be death to this poor XXXXX if he lost the letter , and that at all hazards she must put it back again .	officer		attendant|chatter|curiosity|hold|letter|man|officer|servants|stout|travel

1 So Nur Mahomed handed over his horse to an attendant , and wandered down into the lovely gardens he had seen from the road , and sat down in the shade to rest himself .
2 He flung himself on his back and watched the birds twittering and chattering in the trees above him .
3 Through the branches he could see great patches of sky where the kites wheeled and circled incessantly , with shrill whistling cried .
4 Bees buzzed over the flowers with a soothing sound , and in a few minutes Nur Mahomed was fast asleep .
5 Every day , through the heat of the afternoon , the governor , and his wife also , used to lie down for two or three hours in their own rooms , and so , for the matter of that , did most people in the palace .
6 But the princess , like many other girls , was restless , and preferred to wander about the garden , rather than rest on a pile of soft cushions .
7 What a torment her stout old attendants and servants sometime thought her when she insisted on staying awake , and making them chatter or do something , when they could hardly keep their eyes open !

4 But this was not so easy ; and , moreover , the letter in her hand burnt her with longing to read it , and see what was inside .
5 She examined the seal .
6 It was sticky with being exposed to the hot sun , and with a very little effort it parted from the paper .
7 The letter was open and she read it !
8 And this was what was written : ` Behead the messenger who brings this letter secretly and at once .
9 Ask no questions . '
10 The girl grew pale .
11 What a shame !
12 she thought .
13 SHE would not let a handsome young fellow like that be beheaded ; but how to prevent it was not quite clear at the moment .
14 Some plan must be invented , and she wished to lock herself in where no one could interrupt her , as might easily happen in the garden .
15 So she crept softly to her room , and took a piece of paper and wrote upon it : ` Marry the messenger who brings this letter to the princess openly at once .
16 Ask no questions . '
17 And even contrived to work the seals off the original letter and to fix them to this , so that no one could tell , unless they examined it closely , that it had ever been opened .
18 Then she slipped back , shaking with fear and excitement , to where the young officer still lay asleep , thrust the letter into the fold so his turban , and hurried back to her room .
19 It was done !
20 Late in the afternoon Nur Mahomed woke , and , making sure that the precious despatch was still safe , went off to get ready for his audience with the governor .
21 As soon as he was ushered into his presence he took the letter from his XXXXX and placed it in the governor 's hands according to orders .	turban		SHE|effort|fellow|girl|hazards|moment|paper|seals|sun|turban

18 I know how to obey orders , ' he said , ` and these are as plain as the nose on my face ! '
19 So the princess was summoned , and , somewhat to their surprise , she seemed to take the news very calmly ; next Nur Mahomed was informed , and he was greatly startled , but of course he could but be delighted at the great and unexpected honour which he thought the king had done him .
20 Then all the castle was turned upside down ; and when the news spread in the town , THAT was turned upside down too .

1 Some plan must be invented , and she wished to lock herself in where no one could interrupt her , as might easily happen in the garden .
2 So she crept softly to her room , and took a piece of paper and wrote upon it : ` Marry the messenger who brings this letter to the princess openly at once .
3 Ask no questions . '

6 It was done !

8 As soon as he was ushered into his presence he took the letter from his turban and placed it in the governor 's hands according to orders .

18 So the princess was summoned , and , somewhat to their surprise , she seemed to take the news very calmly ; next Nur Mahomed was informed , and he was greatly startled , but of course he could but be delighted at the great and unexpected honour which he thought the king had done him .

20 Everybody ran everywhere , and tried to do everything at once ; and , in the middle of it all , the old governor went about with his hair standing on end , muttering something about ` obeying orders . '

1 Ask no questions . '
2 And even contrived to work the seals off the original letter and to fix them to this , so that no one could tell , unless they examined it closely , that it had ever been opened .
3 Then she slipped back , shaking with fear and excitement , to where the young officer still lay asleep , thrust the letter into the fold so his turban , and hurried back to her room .
4 It was done !
5 Late in the afternoon Nur Mahomed woke , and , making sure that the precious despatch was still safe , went off to get ready for his audience with the governor .
6 As soon as he was ushered into his presence he took the letter from his turban and placed it in the governor 's hands according to orders .
7 When he had read it the governor was certainly a little astonished ; but he was told in the letter to ` ask no questions , ' and he knew how to obey orders .
8 He sent for his wife and told her to get the princess ready to be married at once .
9 ` Nonsense ! '
10 said his wife , ` what in the world do you mean ? '
11 ` These are the king 's commands , ' he answered ; ` go and do as I bid you .
12 The letter says `` at once , '' and `` ask no questions . ''
13 The marriage , therefore , must take place this evening . '
14 In vain did his wife urge every objection ; the more she argued , the more determined was her husband . '
15 I know how to obey orders , ' he said , ` and these are as plain as the nose on my face ! '
16 So the princess was summoned , and , somewhat to their surprise , she seemed to take the news very calmly ; next Nur Mahomed was informed , and he was greatly startled , but of course he could but be delighted at the great and unexpected honour which he thought the king had done him .
17 Then all the castle was turned upside down ; and when the news spread in the town , THAT was turned upside down too .
18 Everybody ran everywhere , and tried to do everything at once ; and , in the middle of it all , the old governor went about with his hair standing on end , muttering something about ` obeying orders . '
19 And so the marriage was celebrated , and there was a great feast in the castle , and another in the soldiers ' barracks , and illuminations all over the town and in the beautiful gardens .
20 And all the people declared that such a wonderful sight had never been seen , and talked about it to the ends of their lives .
21 The next day the XXXXX despatched the princess and her bridegroom to the king , with a troop of horsemen , splendidly dressed , and he sent a mounted messenger on before them , with a letter giving the account of the marriage to the king .	governor		Nonsense|course|evening|excitement|governor|illuminations|princess|soldiers|town|wife

21 Then , after awhile , he began to think he had better make the best of it , especially as the old governor had been clever enough to send him back his letter , and the king was pretty sure that this was in the XXXXX 's handwriting .	princess		day|everything|face|honour|husband|middle|orders|place|princess|surprise

1 As soon as he was ushered into his presence he took the letter from his turban and placed it in the governor 's hands according to orders .
2 When he had read it the governor was certainly a little astonished ; but he was told in the letter to ` ask no questions , ' and he knew how to obey orders .
3 He sent for his wife and told her to get the princess ready to be married at once .
4 ` Nonsense ! '
5 said his wife , ` what in the world do you mean ? '
6 ` These are the king 's commands , ' he answered ; ` go and do as I bid you .
7 The letter says `` at once , '' and `` ask no questions . ''
8 The marriage , therefore , must take place this evening . '
9 In vain did his wife urge every objection ; the more she argued , the more determined was her husband . '
10 I know how to obey orders , ' he said , ` and these are as plain as the nose on my face ! '
11 So the princess was summoned , and , somewhat to their surprise , she seemed to take the news very calmly ; next Nur Mahomed was informed , and he was greatly startled , but of course he could but be delighted at the great and unexpected honour which he thought the king had done him .
12 Then all the castle was turned upside down ; and when the news spread in the town , THAT was turned upside down too .
13 Everybody ran everywhere , and tried to do everything at once ; and , in the middle of it all , the old governor went about with his hair standing on end , muttering something about ` obeying orders . '
14 And so the marriage was celebrated , and there was a great feast in the castle , and another in the soldiers ' barracks , and illuminations all over the town and in the beautiful gardens .
15 And all the people declared that such a wonderful sight had never been seen , and talked about it to the ends of their lives .
16 The next day the governor despatched the princess and her bridegroom to the king , with a troop of horsemen , splendidly dressed , and he sent a mounted messenger on before them , with a letter giving the account of the marriage to the king .
17 When the king got the governor 's letter , he grew so red in the face that everyone thought he was going to have apoplexy .
18 They were all very anxious to know what had happened , but he rushed off and locked himself into a room , where he ramped and raved until he was tired .
19 Then , after awhile , he began to think he had better make the best of it , especially as the old governor had been clever enough to send him back his letter , and the king was pretty sure that this was in the princess 's handwriting .
20 He was fond of his daughter , and though she had behaved badly , he did not wish to cut HER head off , and he did not want people to know the truth because it would make him look foolish .
21 In fact , the more he considered the matter , the more he felt that he would be wise to put a good XXXXX on it , and to let people suppose that he had really brought about the marriage of his own free will .	face		bridegroom|course|evening|face|husband|place|princess|questions|surprise|turban

16 He was fond of his daughter , and though she had behaved badly , he did not wish to cut HER head off , and he did not want people to know the truth because it would make him look foolish .
17 In fact , the more he considered the matter , the more he felt that he would be wise to put a good face on it , and to let people suppose that he had really brought about the marriage of his own free will .
18 So , when the young couple arrived , the king received them with all state , and gave his son-in-law a province to govern .
19 Nur Mahomed soon proved himself as able and honourable a governor as he was a brave soldier ; and , when the old king died , he became king in his place , and reigned long and happily .
20 Nur Mahomed 's old mother lived for a long time in her ` son 's ' palace , and died in peace .
21 The princess , his wife , although she had got her XXXXX by a trick , found that she could not trick HIM , and so she never tried , but busied herself in teaching her children and scolding her maids .	husband		commands|couple|course|husband|marriage|middle|mother|news|objection|princess

1 ` These are the king 's commands , ' he answered ; ` go and do as I bid you .
2 The letter says `` at once , '' and `` ask no questions . ''
3 The marriage , therefore , must take place this evening . '
4 In vain did his wife urge every objection ; the more she argued , the more determined was her husband . '
5 I know how to obey orders , ' he said , ` and these are as plain as the nose on my face ! '
6 So the princess was summoned , and , somewhat to their surprise , she seemed to take the news very calmly ; next Nur Mahomed was informed , and he was greatly startled , but of course he could but be delighted at the great and unexpected honour which he thought the king had done him .
7 Then all the castle was turned upside down ; and when the news spread in the town , THAT was turned upside down too .
8 Everybody ran everywhere , and tried to do everything at once ; and , in the middle of it all , the old governor went about with his hair standing on end , muttering something about ` obeying orders . '
9 And so the marriage was celebrated , and there was a great feast in the castle , and another in the soldiers ' barracks , and illuminations all over the town and in the beautiful gardens .
10 And all the people declared that such a wonderful sight had never been seen , and talked about it to the ends of their lives .
11 The next day the governor despatched the princess and her bridegroom to the king , with a troop of horsemen , splendidly dressed , and he sent a mounted messenger on before them , with a letter giving the account of the marriage to the king .
12 When the king got the governor 's letter , he grew so red in the face that everyone thought he was going to have apoplexy .
13 They were all very anxious to know what had happened , but he rushed off and locked himself into a room , where he ramped and raved until he was tired .
14 Then , after awhile , he began to think he had better make the best of it , especially as the old governor had been clever enough to send him back his letter , and the king was pretty sure that this was in the princess 's handwriting .
15 He was fond of his daughter , and though she had behaved badly , he did not wish to cut HER head off , and he did not want people to know the truth because it would make him look foolish .
16 In fact , the more he considered the matter , the more he felt that he would be wise to put a good face on it , and to let people suppose that he had really brought about the marriage of his own free will .
17 So , when the young couple arrived , the king received them with all state , and gave his son-in-law a province to govern .
18 Nur Mahomed soon proved himself as able and honourable a governor as he was a brave soldier ; and , when the old king died , he became king in his place , and reigned long and happily .
19 Nur Mahomed 's old mother lived for a long time in her ` son 's ' palace , and died in peace .
20 The princess , his wife , although she had got her husband by a trick , found that she could not trick HIM , and so she never tried , but busied herself in teaching her children and scolding her maids .
21 As for the old hermit , no trace of him was ever discovered ; but the cave is there , and the leaves lie thick in front of it unto this XXXXX .	day		commands|couple|course|day|evening|handwriting|king|lives|nose|palace

1 The marriage , therefore , must take place this evening . '
2 In vain did his wife urge every objection ; the more she argued , the more determined was her husband . '
3 I know how to obey orders , ' he said , ` and these are as plain as the nose on my face ! '
4 So the princess was summoned , and , somewhat to their surprise , she seemed to take the news very calmly ; next Nur Mahomed was informed , and he was greatly startled , but of course he could but be delighted at the great and unexpected honour which he thought the king had done him .
5 Then all the castle was turned upside down ; and when the news spread in the town , THAT was turned upside down too .
6 Everybody ran everywhere , and tried to do everything at once ; and , in the middle of it all , the old governor went about with his hair standing on end , muttering something about ` obeying orders . '
7 And so the marriage was celebrated , and there was a great feast in the castle , and another in the soldiers ' barracks , and illuminations all over the town and in the beautiful gardens .
8 And all the people declared that such a wonderful sight had never been seen , and talked about it to the ends of their lives .
9 The next day the governor despatched the princess and her bridegroom to the king , with a troop of horsemen , splendidly dressed , and he sent a mounted messenger on before them , with a letter giving the account of the marriage to the king .
10 When the king got the governor 's letter , he grew so red in the face that everyone thought he was going to have apoplexy .
11 They were all very anxious to know what had happened , but he rushed off and locked himself into a room , where he ramped and raved until he was tired .
12 Then , after awhile , he began to think he had better make the best of it , especially as the old governor had been clever enough to send him back his letter , and the king was pretty sure that this was in the princess 's handwriting .
13 He was fond of his daughter , and though she had behaved badly , he did not wish to cut HER head off , and he did not want people to know the truth because it would make him look foolish .
14 In fact , the more he considered the matter , the more he felt that he would be wise to put a good face on it , and to let people suppose that he had really brought about the marriage of his own free will .
15 So , when the young couple arrived , the king received them with all state , and gave his son-in-law a province to govern .
16 Nur Mahomed soon proved himself as able and honourable a governor as he was a brave soldier ; and , when the old king died , he became king in his place , and reigned long and happily .
17 Nur Mahomed 's old mother lived for a long time in her ` son 's ' palace , and died in peace .
18 The princess , his wife , although she had got her husband by a trick , found that she could not trick HIM , and so she never tried , but busied herself in teaching her children and scolding her maids .
19 As for the old hermit , no trace of him was ever discovered ; but the cave is there , and the leaves lie thick in front of it unto this day .

1 I know how to obey orders , ' he said , ` and these are as plain as the nose on my face ! '
2 So the princess was summoned , and , somewhat to their surprise , she seemed to take the news very calmly ; next Nur Mahomed was informed , and he was greatly startled , but of course he could but be delighted at the great and unexpected honour which he thought the king had done him .
3 Then all the castle was turned upside down ; and when the news spread in the town , THAT was turned upside down too .
4 Everybody ran everywhere , and tried to do everything at once ; and , in the middle of it all , the old governor went about with his hair standing on end , muttering something about ` obeying orders . '
5 And so the marriage was celebrated , and there was a great feast in the castle , and another in the soldiers ' barracks , and illuminations all over the town and in the beautiful gardens .
6 And all the people declared that such a wonderful sight had never been seen , and talked about it to the ends of their lives .
7 The next day the governor despatched the princess and her bridegroom to the king , with a troop of horsemen , splendidly dressed , and he sent a mounted messenger on before them , with a letter giving the account of the marriage to the king .
8 When the king got the governor 's letter , he grew so red in the face that everyone thought he was going to have apoplexy .
9 They were all very anxious to know what had happened , but he rushed off and locked himself into a room , where he ramped and raved until he was tired .
10 Then , after awhile , he began to think he had better make the best of it , especially as the old governor had been clever enough to send him back his letter , and the king was pretty sure that this was in the princess 's handwriting .
11 He was fond of his daughter , and though she had behaved badly , he did not wish to cut HER head off , and he did not want people to know the truth because it would make him look foolish .
12 In fact , the more he considered the matter , the more he felt that he would be wise to put a good face on it , and to let people suppose that he had really brought about the marriage of his own free will .
13 So , when the young couple arrived , the king received them with all state , and gave his son-in-law a province to govern .
14 Nur Mahomed soon proved himself as able and honourable a governor as he was a brave soldier ; and , when the old king died , he became king in his place , and reigned long and happily .
15 Nur Mahomed 's old mother lived for a long time in her ` son 's ' palace , and died in peace .
16 The princess , his wife , although she had got her husband by a trick , found that she could not trick HIM , and so she never tried , but busied herself in teaching her children and scolding her maids .
17 As for the old hermit , no trace of him was ever discovered ; but the cave is there , and the leaves lie thick in front of it unto this day .

10 Story of Wali Dad the Simple-Hearted Once upon a time there lived a poor old man whose name was Wali Dad Gunjay , or Wali Dad the Bald .
11 He had no relations , but lived all by himself in a little mud hut some distance from any town , and made his living by cutting grass in the jungle , and selling it as fodder for horses .
12 He only earned by this five halfpence a day ; but he was a simple old man , and needed so little out of it , that he saved up one halfpenny daily , and spent the rest upon such food and clothing as he required .
13 In this way he lived for many years until , one night , he thought that he would count the money he had hidden away in the great earthen pot under the floor of his hut .
14 So he set to work , and with much trouble he pulled the bag out on to the floor , and sat gazing in astonishment at the heap of coins which tumbled out of it .
15 What should he do with them all ?
16 he wondered .
17 But he never thought of spending the money on himself , because he was content to pass the rest of his days as he had been doing for ever so long , and he really had no desire for any greater comfort or luxury .
18 At last he threw all the money into an old sack , which he pushed under his bead , and then , rolled in his ragged old blanket , he went off to sleep .
19 Early next morning he staggered off with his sack of money to the shop of a jeweller , whom he knew in the town , and bargained with him for a beautiful little gold bracelet .
20 With this carefully wrapped up in his cotton waistband he went to the house of a rich friend , who was a travelling merchant , and used to wander about with his camels and merchandise through many countries .
21 Wali Dad was lucky enough to find him at home , so he sat down , and after a little talk he asked the XXXXX who was the most virtuous and beautiful lady he had ever met with .	merchant		countries|face|front|house|merchant|name|princess|sack|trick|writer

5 Nur Mahomed 's old mother lived for a long time in her ` son 's ' palace , and died in peace .
6 The princess , his wife , although she had got her husband by a trick , found that she could not trick HIM , and so she never tried , but busied herself in teaching her children and scolding her maids .
7 As for the old hermit , no trace of him was ever discovered ; but the cave is there , and the leaves lie thick in front of it unto this day .
8 -LSB- Told the writer by an Indian . -RSB-
9 Story of Wali Dad the Simple-Hearted Once upon a time there lived a poor old man whose name was Wali Dad Gunjay , or Wali Dad the Bald .
10 He had no relations , but lived all by himself in a little mud hut some distance from any town , and made his living by cutting grass in the jungle , and selling it as fodder for horses .
11 He only earned by this five halfpence a day ; but he was a simple old man , and needed so little out of it , that he saved up one halfpenny daily , and spent the rest upon such food and clothing as he required .
12 In this way he lived for many years until , one night , he thought that he would count the money he had hidden away in the great earthen pot under the floor of his hut .
13 So he set to work , and with much trouble he pulled the bag out on to the floor , and sat gazing in astonishment at the heap of coins which tumbled out of it .
14 What should he do with them all ?
15 he wondered .
16 But he never thought of spending the money on himself , because he was content to pass the rest of his days as he had been doing for ever so long , and he really had no desire for any greater comfort or luxury .
17 At last he threw all the money into an old sack , which he pushed under his bead , and then , rolled in his ragged old blanket , he went off to sleep .
18 Early next morning he staggered off with his sack of money to the shop of a jeweller , whom he knew in the town , and bargained with him for a beautiful little gold bracelet .
19 With this carefully wrapped up in his cotton waistband he went to the house of a rich friend , who was a travelling merchant , and used to wander about with his camels and merchandise through many countries .
20 Wali Dad was lucky enough to find him at home , so he sat down , and after a little talk he asked the merchant who was the most virtuous and beautiful lady he had ever met with .
21 The merchant replied that the XXXXX of Khaistan was renowned everywhere as well for the beauty of her person as for the kindness and generosity of her disposition .	princess		bead|children|couple|daughter|desire|front|governor|maids|man|princess

2 So , when the young couple arrived , the king received them with all state , and gave his son-in-law a province to govern .
3 Nur Mahomed soon proved himself as able and honourable a governor as he was a brave soldier ; and , when the old king died , he became king in his place , and reigned long and happily .
4 Nur Mahomed 's old mother lived for a long time in her ` son 's ' palace , and died in peace .
5 The princess , his wife , although she had got her husband by a trick , found that she could not trick HIM , and so she never tried , but busied herself in teaching her children and scolding her maids .
6 As for the old hermit , no trace of him was ever discovered ; but the cave is there , and the leaves lie thick in front of it unto this day .

11 At last he threw all the money into an old sack , which he pushed under his bead , and then , rolled in his ragged old blanket , he went off to sleep .
12 Early next morning he staggered off with his sack of money to the shop of a jeweller , whom he knew in the town , and bargained with him for a beautiful little gold bracelet .
13 With this carefully wrapped up in his cotton waistband he went to the house of a rich friend , who was a travelling merchant , and used to wander about with his camels and merchandise through many countries .
14 Wali Dad was lucky enough to find him at home , so he sat down , and after a little talk he asked the merchant who was the most virtuous and beautiful lady he had ever met with .
15 The merchant replied that the princess of Khaistan was renowned everywhere as well for the beauty of her person as for the kindness and generosity of her disposition .
16 ` Then , ' said Wali Dad , ` next time you go that way , give her this little bracelet , with the respectful compliments of one who admires virtue far more than he desires wealth . '
17 With that he pulled the bracelet from his waistband , and handed it to his friend .
18 The merchant was naturally much astonished , but said nothing , and made no objection to carrying out his friend 's plan .
19 Time passed by , and at length the merchant arrived in the course of his travels at the capital of Khaistan .
20 As soon as he had opportunity he presented himself at the palace , and sent in the bracelet , neatly packed in a little perfumed box provided by himself , giving at the same time the message entrusted to him by Wali Dad .
21 The XXXXX could not think who could have bestowed this present on her , but she bade her servant to tell the merchant that if he would return , after he had finished his business in the city , she would give him her reply .	princess		Bald|bracelet|capital|cave|cotton|food|friend|person|princess|writer

3 Story of Wali Dad the Simple-Hearted Once upon a time there lived a poor old man whose name was Wali Dad Gunjay , or Wali Dad the Bald .
4 He had no relations , but lived all by himself in a little mud hut some distance from any town , and made his living by cutting grass in the jungle , and selling it as fodder for horses .
5 He only earned by this five halfpence a day ; but he was a simple old man , and needed so little out of it , that he saved up one halfpenny daily , and spent the rest upon such food and clothing as he required .
6 In this way he lived for many years until , one night , he thought that he would count the money he had hidden away in the great earthen pot under the floor of his hut .
7 So he set to work , and with much trouble he pulled the bag out on to the floor , and sat gazing in astonishment at the heap of coins which tumbled out of it .
8 What should he do with them all ?
9 he wondered .
10 But he never thought of spending the money on himself , because he was content to pass the rest of his days as he had been doing for ever so long , and he really had no desire for any greater comfort or luxury .
11 At last he threw all the money into an old sack , which he pushed under his bead , and then , rolled in his ragged old blanket , he went off to sleep .
12 Early next morning he staggered off with his sack of money to the shop of a jeweller , whom he knew in the town , and bargained with him for a beautiful little gold bracelet .
13 With this carefully wrapped up in his cotton waistband he went to the house of a rich friend , who was a travelling merchant , and used to wander about with his camels and merchandise through many countries .
14 Wali Dad was lucky enough to find him at home , so he sat down , and after a little talk he asked the merchant who was the most virtuous and beautiful lady he had ever met with .
15 The merchant replied that the princess of Khaistan was renowned everywhere as well for the beauty of her person as for the kindness and generosity of her disposition .
16 ` Then , ' said Wali Dad , ` next time you go that way , give her this little bracelet , with the respectful compliments of one who admires virtue far more than he desires wealth . '
17 With that he pulled the bracelet from his waistband , and handed it to his friend .
18 The merchant was naturally much astonished , but said nothing , and made no objection to carrying out his friend 's plan .
19 Time passed by , and at length the merchant arrived in the course of his travels at the capital of Khaistan .
20 As soon as he had opportunity he presented himself at the palace , and sent in the bracelet , neatly packed in a little perfumed box provided by himself , giving at the same time the message entrusted to him by Wali Dad .

2 Story of Wali Dad the Simple-Hearted Once upon a time there lived a poor old man whose name was Wali Dad Gunjay , or Wali Dad the Bald .
3 He had no relations , but lived all by himself in a little mud hut some distance from any town , and made his living by cutting grass in the jungle , and selling it as fodder for horses .
4 He only earned by this five halfpence a day ; but he was a simple old man , and needed so little out of it , that he saved up one halfpenny daily , and spent the rest upon such food and clothing as he required .
5 In this way he lived for many years until , one night , he thought that he would count the money he had hidden away in the great earthen pot under the floor of his hut .
6 So he set to work , and with much trouble he pulled the bag out on to the floor , and sat gazing in astonishment at the heap of coins which tumbled out of it .
7 What should he do with them all ?
8 he wondered .
9 But he never thought of spending the money on himself , because he was content to pass the rest of his days as he had been doing for ever so long , and he really had no desire for any greater comfort or luxury .

12 With this carefully wrapped up in his cotton waistband he went to the house of a rich friend , who was a travelling merchant , and used to wander about with his camels and merchandise through many countries .
13 Wali Dad was lucky enough to find him at home , so he sat down , and after a little talk he asked the merchant who was the most virtuous and beautiful lady he had ever met with .
14 The merchant replied that the princess of Khaistan was renowned everywhere as well for the beauty of her person as for the kindness and generosity of her disposition .

20 The princess could not think who could have bestowed this present on her , but she bade her servant to tell the merchant that if he would return , after he had finished his business in the city , she would give him her reply .

21 ` Of course , ' cried the XXXXX , greatly amused ; ` from Delhi to Baghdad , and from Constantinople to Lucknow , I know them all ; and there lives none worthier than the gallant and wealthy young prince of Nekabad . '	merchant		bead|bracelet|cotton|length|luxury|merchant|perplexity|shape|thought|virtue

1 What should he do with them all ?
2 he wondered .
3 But he never thought of spending the money on himself , because he was content to pass the rest of his days as he had been doing for ever so long , and he really had no desire for any greater comfort or luxury .
4 At last he threw all the money into an old sack , which he pushed under his bead , and then , rolled in his ragged old blanket , he went off to sleep .
5 Early next morning he staggered off with his sack of money to the shop of a jeweller , whom he knew in the town , and bargained with him for a beautiful little gold bracelet .
6 With this carefully wrapped up in his cotton waistband he went to the house of a rich friend , who was a travelling merchant , and used to wander about with his camels and merchandise through many countries .
7 Wali Dad was lucky enough to find him at home , so he sat down , and after a little talk he asked the merchant who was the most virtuous and beautiful lady he had ever met with .
8 The merchant replied that the princess of Khaistan was renowned everywhere as well for the beauty of her person as for the kindness and generosity of her disposition .
9 ` Then , ' said Wali Dad , ` next time you go that way , give her this little bracelet , with the respectful compliments of one who admires virtue far more than he desires wealth . '
10 With that he pulled the bracelet from his waistband , and handed it to his friend .
11 The merchant was naturally much astonished , but said nothing , and made no objection to carrying out his friend 's plan .
12 Time passed by , and at length the merchant arrived in the course of his travels at the capital of Khaistan .
13 As soon as he had opportunity he presented himself at the palace , and sent in the bracelet , neatly packed in a little perfumed box provided by himself , giving at the same time the message entrusted to him by Wali Dad .
14 The princess could not think who could have bestowed this present on her , but she bade her servant to tell the merchant that if he would return , after he had finished his business in the city , she would give him her reply .
15 In a few days , therefore , the merchant came back , and received from the princess a return present in the shape of a camel-load or rich silks , besides a present of money for himself .
16 With these he set out on his journey .
17 Some months later he got home again from his journeyings , and proceeded to take Wali Dad the princess 's present .
18 Great was the perplexity of the good man to find a camel-load of silks tumbled at his door !
19 What was he to do with these costly things ?
20 But , presently , after much thought , he begged the merchant to consider whether he did not know of some young prince to whom such treasures might be useful .
21 ` Of course , ' cried the merchant , greatly amused ; ` from Delhi to Baghdad , and from Constantinople to Lucknow , I know them all ; and there lives none worthier than the gallant and wealthy young XXXXX of Nekabad . 20 ` Very well , then , take the silks to him , with the blessing of an old man , ' said Wali Dad , much relieved to be rid of them .

2 With this carefully wrapped up in his cotton waistband he went to the house of a rich friend , who was a travelling merchant , and used to wander about with his camels and merchandise through many countries .
3 Wali Dad was lucky enough to find him at home , so he sat down , and after a little talk he asked the merchant who was the most virtuous and beautiful lady he had ever met with .
4 The merchant replied that the princess of Khaistan was renowned everywhere as well for the beauty of her person as for the kindness and generosity of her disposition .
5 ` Then , ' said Wali Dad , ` next time you go that way , give her this little bracelet , with the respectful compliments of one who admires virtue far more than he desires wealth . '
6 With that he pulled the bracelet from his waistband , and handed it to his friend .
7 The merchant was naturally much astonished , but said nothing , and made no objection to carrying out his friend 's plan .
8 Time passed by , and at length the merchant arrived in the course of his travels at the capital of Khaistan .
9 As soon as he had opportunity he presented himself at the palace , and sent in the bracelet , neatly packed in a little perfumed box provided by himself , giving at the same time the message entrusted to him by Wali Dad .
10 The princess could not think who could have bestowed this present on her , but she bade her servant to tell the merchant that if he would return , after he had finished his business in the city , she would give him her reply .
11 In a few days , therefore , the merchant came back , and received from the princess a return present in the shape of a camel-load or rich silks , besides a present of money for himself .
12 With these he set out on his journey .
13 Some months later he got home again from his journeyings , and proceeded to take Wali Dad the princess 's present .
14 Great was the perplexity of the good man to find a camel-load of silks tumbled at his door !
15 What was he to do with these costly things ?
16 But , presently , after much thought , he begged the merchant to consider whether he did not know of some young prince to whom such treasures might be useful .
17 ` Of course , ' cried the merchant , greatly amused ; ` from Delhi to Baghdad , and from Constantinople to Lucknow , I know them all ; and there lives none worthier than the gallant and wealthy young prince of Nekabad . '
18 ` Very well , then , take the silks to him , with the blessing of an old man , ' said Wali Dad , much relieved to be rid of them .
19 So , the next time that the merchant journeyed that way he carried the silks with him , and in due course arrived at Nekabad , and sought an audience of the prince .
20 When he was shown into his presence he produced the beautiful gift of silks that Wali Dad had sent , and begged the young man to accept them as a humble tribute to his worth and greatness .
21 The prince was much touched by the generosity of the giver , and ordered , as a XXXXX present , twelve of the finest breed of horses for which his country was famous to be delivered over to the merchant , to whom also , before he took his leave , he gave a munificent reward for his services .	return		audience|bracelet|compliments|disposition|home|presence|return|servant|talk|thought

2 Wali Dad was lucky enough to find him at home , so he sat down , and after a little talk he asked the merchant who was the most virtuous and beautiful lady he had ever met with .
3 The merchant replied that the princess of Khaistan was renowned everywhere as well for the beauty of her person as for the kindness and generosity of her disposition .
4 ` Then , ' said Wali Dad , ` next time you go that way , give her this little bracelet , with the respectful compliments of one who admires virtue far more than he desires wealth . '
5 With that he pulled the bracelet from his waistband , and handed it to his friend .
6 The merchant was naturally much astonished , but said nothing , and made no objection to carrying out his friend 's plan .
7 Time passed by , and at length the merchant arrived in the course of his travels at the capital of Khaistan .
8 As soon as he had opportunity he presented himself at the palace , and sent in the bracelet , neatly packed in a little perfumed box provided by himself , giving at the same time the message entrusted to him by Wali Dad .
9 The princess could not think who could have bestowed this present on her , but she bade her servant to tell the merchant that if he would return , after he had finished his business in the city , she would give him her reply .
10 In a few days , therefore , the merchant came back , and received from the princess a return present in the shape of a camel-load or rich silks , besides a present of money for himself .
11 With these he set out on his journey .
12 Some months later he got home again from his journeyings , and proceeded to take Wali Dad the princess 's present .
13 Great was the perplexity of the good man to find a camel-load of silks tumbled at his door !
14 What was he to do with these costly things ?
15 But , presently , after much thought , he begged the merchant to consider whether he did not know of some young prince to whom such treasures might be useful .
16 ` Of course , ' cried the merchant , greatly amused ; ` from Delhi to Baghdad , and from Constantinople to Lucknow , I know them all ; and there lives none worthier than the gallant and wealthy young prince of Nekabad . '
17 ` Very well , then , take the silks to him , with the blessing of an old man , ' said Wali Dad , much relieved to be rid of them .
18 So , the next time that the merchant journeyed that way he carried the silks with him , and in due course arrived at Nekabad , and sought an audience of the prince .
19 When he was shown into his presence he produced the beautiful gift of silks that Wali Dad had sent , and begged the young man to accept them as a humble tribute to his worth and greatness .
20 The prince was much touched by the generosity of the giver , and ordered , as a return present , twelve of the finest breed of horses for which his country was famous to be delivered over to the merchant , to whom also , before he took his leave , he gave a munificent reward for his services .

2 In a few days , therefore , the merchant came back , and received from the princess a return present in the shape of a camel-load or rich silks , besides a present of money for himself .
3 With these he set out on his journey .
4 Some months later he got home again from his journeyings , and proceeded to take Wali Dad the princess 's present .
5 Great was the perplexity of the good man to find a camel-load of silks tumbled at his door !
6 What was he to do with these costly things ?
7 But , presently , after much thought , he begged the merchant to consider whether he did not know of some young prince to whom such treasures might be useful .
8 ` Of course , ' cried the merchant , greatly amused ; ` from Delhi to Baghdad , and from Constantinople to Lucknow , I know them all ; and there lives none worthier than the gallant and wealthy young prince of Nekabad . '
9 ` Very well , then , take the silks to him , with the blessing of an old man , ' said Wali Dad , much relieved to be rid of them .
10 So , the next time that the merchant journeyed that way he carried the silks with him , and in due course arrived at Nekabad , and sought an audience of the prince .
11 When he was shown into his presence he produced the beautiful gift of silks that Wali Dad had sent , and begged the young man to accept them as a humble tribute to his worth and greatness .
12 The prince was much touched by the generosity of the giver , and ordered , as a return present , twelve of the finest breed of horses for which his country was famous to be delivered over to the merchant , to whom also , before he took his leave , he gave a munificent reward for his services .
13 As before , the merchant at last arrived at home ; and next day , he set out for Wali Dad 's house with the twelve horses .
14 When the old man saw them coming in the distance he said to himself : ` Here 's luck !
15 a troop of horses coming !
16 They are sure to want quantities of grass , and I shall sell all I have without having to drag it to market . '
17 Thereupon he rushed off and cut grass as fast he could .
18 When he got back , with as much grass as he could possibly carry , he was greatly discomfited to find that the horses were all for himself .
19 At first he could not think what to do with them , but , after a little , a brilliant idea struck him !
20 He gave two to the merchant , and begged him to take the rest to the princess of Khaistan , who was clearly the fittest person to possess such beautiful animals .

18 At first he could not think what to do with them , but , after a little , a brilliant idea struck him !
19 He gave two to the merchant , and begged him to take the rest to the princess of Khaistan , who was clearly the fittest person to possess such beautiful animals .
20 The merchant departed , laughing .
21 But , true to his old friend 's request , he took the XXXXX with him on his next journey , and eventually presented them safely to the princess .	horses		Great|door|grass|horses|luck|none|prince|princess|shape|thought

1 In a few days , therefore , the merchant came back , and received from the princess a return present in the shape of a camel-load or rich silks , besides a present of money for himself .
2 With these he set out on his journey .
3 Some months later he got home again from his journeyings , and proceeded to take Wali Dad the princess 's present .
4 Great was the perplexity of the good man to find a camel-load of silks tumbled at his door !
5 What was he to do with these costly things ?
6 But , presently , after much thought , he begged the merchant to consider whether he did not know of some young prince to whom such treasures might be useful .
7 ` Of course , ' cried the merchant , greatly amused ; ` from Delhi to Baghdad , and from Constantinople to Lucknow , I know them all ; and there lives none worthier than the gallant and wealthy young prince of Nekabad . '
8 ` Very well , then , take the silks to him , with the blessing of an old man , ' said Wali Dad , much relieved to be rid of them .
9 So , the next time that the merchant journeyed that way he carried the silks with him , and in due course arrived at Nekabad , and sought an audience of the prince .
10 When he was shown into his presence he produced the beautiful gift of silks that Wali Dad had sent , and begged the young man to accept them as a humble tribute to his worth and greatness .
11 The prince was much touched by the generosity of the giver , and ordered , as a return present , twelve of the finest breed of horses for which his country was famous to be delivered over to the merchant , to whom also , before he took his leave , he gave a munificent reward for his services .
12 As before , the merchant at last arrived at home ; and next day , he set out for Wali Dad 's house with the twelve horses .
13 When the old man saw them coming in the distance he said to himself : ` Here 's luck !
14 a troop of horses coming !
15 They are sure to want quantities of grass , and I shall sell all I have without having to drag it to market . '
16 Thereupon he rushed off and cut grass as fast he could .
17 When he got back , with as much grass as he could possibly carry , he was greatly discomfited to find that the horses were all for himself .
18 At first he could not think what to do with them , but , after a little , a brilliant idea struck him !
19 He gave two to the merchant , and begged him to take the rest to the princess of Khaistan , who was clearly the fittest person to possess such beautiful animals .
20 The merchant departed , laughing .
21 But , true to his old friend 's request , he took the horses with him on his next XXXXX , and eventually presented them safely to the princess .	journey		day|distance|giver|home|journey|man|months|return|thought|treasures

16 But , true to his old friend 's request , he took the horses with him on his next journey , and eventually presented them safely to the princess .
17 This time the princess sent for the merchant , and questioned him about the giver .
18 Now , the merchant was usually a most honest man , but he did not quite like to describe Wali Dad in his true light as an old man whose income was five halfpence a day , and who had hardly clothes to cover him .
19 So he told her that his friend had heard stories of her beauty and goodness , and had longed to lay the best he had at her feet .
20 The princess then took her father into her confidence , and begged him to advise her what courtesy she might return to one who persisted in making her such presents .
21 ` Well , ' said the king , ` you can not refuse them ; so the best thing you can do is to send this unknown XXXXX at once a present so magnificent that he is not likely to be able to send you anything better , and so will be ashamed to send anything at all ! 1 ` Of course , ' cried the merchant , greatly amused ; ` from Delhi to Baghdad , and from Constantinople to Lucknow , I know them all ; and there lives none worthier than the gallant and wealthy young prince of Nekabad . '
2 ` Very well , then , take the silks to him , with the blessing of an old man , ' said Wali Dad , much relieved to be rid of them .
3 So , the next time that the merchant journeyed that way he carried the silks with him , and in due course arrived at Nekabad , and sought an audience of the prince .
4 When he was shown into his presence he produced the beautiful gift of silks that Wali Dad had sent , and begged the young man to accept them as a humble tribute to his worth and greatness .
5 The prince was much touched by the generosity of the giver , and ordered , as a return present , twelve of the finest breed of horses for which his country was famous to be delivered over to the merchant , to whom also , before he took his leave , he gave a munificent reward for his services .
6 As before , the merchant at last arrived at home ; and next day , he set out for Wali Dad 's house with the twelve horses .
7 When the old man saw them coming in the distance he said to himself : ` Here 's luck !
8 a troop of horses coming !
9 They are sure to want quantities of grass , and I shall sell all I have without having to drag it to market . '
10 Thereupon he rushed off and cut grass as fast he could .
11 When he got back , with as much grass as he could possibly carry , he was greatly discomfited to find that the horses were all for himself .
12 At first he could not think what to do with them , but , after a little , a brilliant idea struck him !
13 He gave two to the merchant , and begged him to take the rest to the princess of Khaistan , who was clearly the fittest person to possess such beautiful animals .
14 The merchant departed , laughing .
15 But , true to his old friend 's request , he took the horses with him on his next journey , and eventually presented them safely to the princess .
16 This time the princess sent for the merchant , and questioned him about the giver .
17 Now , the merchant was usually a most honest man , but he did not quite like to describe Wali Dad in his true light as an old man whose income was five halfpence a day , and who had hardly clothes to cover him .
18 So he told her that his friend had heard stories of her beauty and goodness , and had longed to lay the best he had at her feet .
19 The princess then took her father into her confidence , and begged him to advise her what courtesy she might return to one who persisted in making her such presents .
20 ` Well , ' said the king , ` you can not refuse them ; so the best thing you can do is to send this unknown friend at once a present so magnificent that he is not likely to be able to send you anything better , and so will be ashamed to send anything at all ! '
21 Then he ordered that , in place of each of the ten XXXXX , two mules laden with silver should be returned by her .	horses		animals|clothes|day|father|horses|income|request|rest|return|tribute

5 They are sure to want quantities of grass , and I shall sell all I have without having to drag it to market . '
6 Thereupon he rushed off and cut grass as fast he could .

8 At first he could not think what to do with them , but , after a little , a brilliant idea struck him !

18 Still , if you will accept six mules and their loads , and will take the rest straight to Nekabad , I shall thank you heartily . '
19 The merchant felt handsomely repaid for his trouble , and wondered greatly how the matter would turn out .
20 So he made no difficulty about it ; and as soon as he could get things ready , he set out for Nekabad with this new and princely gift .

12 ` Well , ' said the king , ` you can not refuse them ; so the best thing you can do is to send this unknown friend at once a present so magnificent that he is not likely to be able to send you anything better , and so will be ashamed to send anything at all ! '
13 Then he ordered that , in place of each of the ten horses , two mules laden with silver should be returned by her .
14 Thus , in a few hours , the merchant found himself in charge of a splendid caravan ; and he had to hire a number of armed men to defend it on the road against the robbers , and he was glad indeed to find himself back again in Wali Dad 's hut .
15 ` Well , now , ' cried Wali Dad , as he viewed all the wealth laid at his door , ' I can well repay that kind prince for his magnificent present of horses ; but to be sure you have been put to great expenses !
16 Still , if you will accept six mules and their loads , and will take the rest straight to Nekabad , I shall thank you heartily . '
17 The merchant felt handsomely repaid for his trouble , and wondered greatly how the matter would turn out .
18 So he made no difficulty about it ; and as soon as he could get things ready , he set out for Nekabad with this new and princely gift .
19 This time the prince , too , was embarrassed , and questioned the merchant closely .
20 The merchant felt that his credit was at stake , and whilst inwardly determining that he would not carry the joke any further , could not help describing Wali Dad in such glowing terms that the old man would never have known himself had he heard them .
21 The prince , like the XXXXX of Khaistan , determined that he would send in return a gift that would be truly royal , and which would perhaps prevent the unknown giver sending him anything more .	king		caravan|confidence|father|gift|income|king|matter|place|stake|time

2 Thereupon he rushed off and cut grass as fast he could .

7 But , true to his old friend 's request , he took the horses with him on his next journey , and eventually presented them safely to the princess .
8 This time the princess sent for the merchant , and questioned him about the giver .
9 Now , the merchant was usually a most honest man , but he did not quite like to describe Wali Dad in his true light as an old man whose income was five halfpence a day , and who had hardly clothes to cover him .

17 here 's a grand crowd coming !
18 Elephants , too !
19 Grass will be selling well to-day ! '
20 And with that he hurried off to the jungle and cut grass as fast as he could .

7 The merchant felt handsomely repaid for his trouble , and wondered greatly how the matter would turn out .
8 So he made no difficulty about it ; and as soon as he could get things ready , he set out for Nekabad with this new and princely gift .
9 This time the prince , too , was embarrassed , and questioned the merchant closely .
10 The merchant felt that his credit was at stake , and whilst inwardly determining that he would not carry the joke any further , could not help describing Wali Dad in such glowing terms that the old man would never have known himself had he heard them .
11 The prince , like the king of Khaistan , determined that he would send in return a gift that would be truly royal , and which would perhaps prevent the unknown giver sending him anything more .
12 So he made up a caravan on twenty splendid horses caparisoned in gold embroidered cloths , with fine morocco saddles and silver bridles and stirrups , also twenty camels of the best breed , which had the speed of race-horses , and could swing along at a trot all day without getting tired ; and , lastly , twenty elephants , with magnificent silver howdahs and coverings of silk embroidered with pearls .
13 To take care of these animals the merchant hired a little army of men ; and the troop made a great show as they travelled along .
14 When Wali Dad from a distance saw the cloud of dust which the caravan made , and the glitter of its appointments , he said to himself : ` By Allah !
15 here 's a grand crowd coming !
16 Elephants , too !
17 Grass will be selling well to-day ! '
18 And with that he hurried off to the jungle and cut grass as fast as he could .
19 As soon as he got back he found the caravan had stopped at his door , and the merchant was waiting , a little anxiously , to tell him the news and to congratulate him upon his riches .
20 ` Riches ! '
21 cried Wali Dad , ` what has an old XXXXX like me with one foot in the grave to do with riches ?	man		Grass|anything|caravan|charge|credit|joke|king|man|thing|time

1 The princess then took her father into her confidence , and begged him to advise her what courtesy she might return to one who persisted in making her such presents .
2 ` Well , ' said the king , ` you can not refuse them ; so the best thing you can do is to send this unknown friend at once a present so magnificent that he is not likely to be able to send you anything better , and so will be ashamed to send anything at all ! '
3 Then he ordered that , in place of each of the ten horses , two mules laden with silver should be returned by her .
4 Thus , in a few hours , the merchant found himself in charge of a splendid caravan ; and he had to hire a number of armed men to defend it on the road against the robbers , and he was glad indeed to find himself back again in Wali Dad 's hut .
5 ` Well , now , ' cried Wali Dad , as he viewed all the wealth laid at his door , ' I can well repay that kind prince for his magnificent present of horses ; but to be sure you have been put to great expenses !
6 Still , if you will accept six mules and their loads , and will take the rest straight to Nekabad , I shall thank you heartily . '
7 The merchant felt handsomely repaid for his trouble , and wondered greatly how the matter would turn out .

8 cried Wali Dad , ` what has an old man like me with one foot in the grave to do with riches ?
9 That beautiful young princess , now !
10 She 'd be the one to enjoy all these fine things !
11 Do you take for yourself two horses , two camels , and two elephants , with all their trappings , and present the rest to her . '
12 The merchant at first objected to these remarks , and pointed out to Wali Dad that he was beginning to feel these embassies a little awkward .
13 Of course he was himself richly repaid , so far as expenses went ; but still he did not like going so often , and he was getting nervous .
14 At length , however he consented to go once more , but he promised himself never to embark on another such enterprise .
15 So , after a few days ' rest , the caravan started off once more for Khaistan .
16 The moment the king of Khaistan saw the gorgeous train of men and beasts entering his palace courtyard , he was so amazed that he hurried down in person to inquire about it , and became dumb when he heard that these also were a present from the princely Wali Dad , and were for the princess , his daughter .
17 He went hastily off to her apartments , and said to her : ' I tell you what it is , my dear , this man wants to marry you ; that is the meaning of all these presents !
18 There is nothing for it but that we go and pay him a visit in person .
19 He must be a man of immense wealth , and as he is so devoted to you , perhaps you might do worse than marry him ! '
20 The princess agreed with all that her father said , and orders were issued for vast numbers of elephants and camels , and gorgeous tents and flags , and litters for the ladies , and horses for the men , to be prepared without delay , as the king and princess were going to pay a visit to the great and munificent prince Wali Dad .
21 The merchant , the XXXXX declared , was to guide the party .	king		camels|distance|jungle|king|length|meaning|merchant|moment|princess|things

17 The feelings of the poor merchant in this sore dilemma can hardly be imagined .
18 Willingly would he have run away ; but he was treated with so much hospitality as Wali Dad 's representative , that he hardly got an instant 's real peace , and never any opportunity of slipping away .
19 In fact , after a few days , despair possessed him to such a degree that he made up his mind that all that happened was fate , and that escape was impossible ; but he hoped devoutly some turn of fortune would reveal to him a way out of the difficulties which he had , with the best intentions , drawn upon himself .

4 The merchant at first objected to these remarks , and pointed out to Wali Dad that he was beginning to feel these embassies a little awkward .
5 Of course he was himself richly repaid , so far as expenses went ; but still he did not like going so often , and he was getting nervous .
6 At length , however he consented to go once more , but he promised himself never to embark on another such enterprise .
7 So , after a few days ' rest , the caravan started off once more for Khaistan .
8 The moment the king of Khaistan saw the gorgeous train of men and beasts entering his palace courtyard , he was so amazed that he hurried down in person to inquire about it , and became dumb when he heard that these also were a present from the princely Wali Dad , and were for the princess , his daughter .
9 He went hastily off to her apartments , and said to her : ' I tell you what it is , my dear , this man wants to marry you ; that is the meaning of all these presents !
10 There is nothing for it but that we go and pay him a visit in person .

13 The merchant , the king declared , was to guide the party .
14 The feelings of the poor merchant in this sore dilemma can hardly be imagined .
15 Willingly would he have run away ; but he was treated with so much hospitality as Wali Dad 's representative , that he hardly got an instant 's real peace , and never any opportunity of slipping away .
16 In fact , after a few days , despair possessed him to such a degree that he made up his mind that all that happened was fate , and that escape was impossible ; but he hoped devoutly some turn of fortune would reveal to him a way out of the difficulties which he had , with the best intentions , drawn upon himself .
17 On the seventh day they all started , amidst thunderous salutes from the ramparts of the city , and much dust , and cheering , and blaring of trumpets .
18 Day after day they moved on , and every day the poor merchant felt more ill and miserable .
19 He wondered what kind of death the king would invent for him , and went through almost as much torture , as he lay awake nearly the whole of every night thinking over the situation , as he would have suffered if the king 's executioners were already setting to work upon his neck .
20 At last they were only one day 's march from Wali Dad 's little mud home .
