12 In fact , after a few days , despair possessed him to such a degree that he made up his mind that all that happened was fate , and that escape was impossible ; but he hoped devoutly some turn of fortune would reveal to him a way out of the difficulties which he had , with the best intentions , drawn upon himself .
13 On the seventh day they all started , amidst thunderous salutes from the ramparts of the city , and much dust , and cheering , and blaring of trumpets .
14 Day after day they moved on , and every day the poor merchant felt more ill and miserable .
15 He wondered what kind of death the king would invent for him , and went through almost as much torture , as he lay awake nearly the whole of every night thinking over the situation , as he would have suffered if the king 's executioners were already setting to work upon his neck .
16 At last they were only one day 's march from Wali Dad 's little mud home .
17 Here a great encampment was made , and the merchant was sent on to tell Wali Dad that the King and Princess of Khaistan had arrived and were seeking an interview .
18 When the merchant arrived he found the poor old man eating his evening meal of onions and dry bread , and when he told him of all that had happened he had not the heart to proceed to load him with the reproaches which rose to his tongue .
19 For Wali Dad was overwhelmed with grief and shame for himself , for his friend , and for the name and honour of the princess ; and he wept and plucked at his beard , and groaned most piteously .
20 With tears he begged the merchant to detain them for one day by any kind of excuse he could think of , and to come in the morning to discuss what they should do .
21 As soon as the merchant was gone Wali Dad made up his mind that there was only one honourable way out of the XXXXX and distress that he had created by his foolishness , and that was -- to kill himself .	shame		beard|blaring|expenses|fact|fate|man|march|meaning|merchant|shame

1 So , after a few days ' rest , the caravan started off once more for Khaistan .
2 The moment the king of Khaistan saw the gorgeous train of men and beasts entering his palace courtyard , he was so amazed that he hurried down in person to inquire about it , and became dumb when he heard that these also were a present from the princely Wali Dad , and were for the princess , his daughter .
3 He went hastily off to her apartments , and said to her : ' I tell you what it is , my dear , this man wants to marry you ; that is the meaning of all these presents !
4 There is nothing for it but that we go and pay him a visit in person .
5 He must be a man of immense wealth , and as he is so devoted to you , perhaps you might do worse than marry him ! '
6 The princess agreed with all that her father said , and orders were issued for vast numbers of elephants and camels , and gorgeous tents and flags , and litters for the ladies , and horses for the men , to be prepared without delay , as the king and princess were going to pay a visit to the great and munificent prince Wali Dad .
7 The merchant , the king declared , was to guide the party .
8 The feelings of the poor merchant in this sore dilemma can hardly be imagined .
9 Willingly would he have run away ; but he was treated with so much hospitality as Wali Dad 's representative , that he hardly got an instant 's real peace , and never any opportunity of slipping away .
10 In fact , after a few days , despair possessed him to such a degree that he made up his mind that all that happened was fate , and that escape was impossible ; but he hoped devoutly some turn of fortune would reveal to him a way out of the difficulties which he had , with the best intentions , drawn upon himself .
11 On the seventh day they all started , amidst thunderous salutes from the ramparts of the city , and much dust , and cheering , and blaring of trumpets .
12 Day after day they moved on , and every day the poor merchant felt more ill and miserable .
13 He wondered what kind of death the king would invent for him , and went through almost as much torture , as he lay awake nearly the whole of every night thinking over the situation , as he would have suffered if the king 's executioners were already setting to work upon his neck .
14 At last they were only one day 's march from Wali Dad 's little mud home .
15 Here a great encampment was made , and the merchant was sent on to tell Wali Dad that the King and Princess of Khaistan had arrived and were seeking an interview .
16 When the merchant arrived he found the poor old man eating his evening meal of onions and dry bread , and when he told him of all that had happened he had not the heart to proceed to load him with the reproaches which rose to his tongue .
17 For Wali Dad was overwhelmed with grief and shame for himself , for his friend , and for the name and honour of the princess ; and he wept and plucked at his beard , and groaned most piteously .
18 With tears he begged the merchant to detain them for one day by any kind of excuse he could think of , and to come in the morning to discuss what they should do .
19 As soon as the merchant was gone Wali Dad made up his mind that there was only one honourable way out of the shame and distress that he had created by his foolishness , and that was -- to kill himself .
20 So , without stopping to ask any one 's advice , he went off in the middle of the night to a place where the river wound along at the base of steep rocky cliffs of great height , and determined to throw himself down and put an end to his life .
21 When he got to the XXXXX he drew back a few paces , took a little run , and at the very edge of that dreadful black gulf he stopped short !	place		beard|caravan|degree|dust|fate|merchant|nothing|place|representative|rest

1 He COULD not do it !
2 From below , unseen in the blackness of the deep night shadows , the water roared and boiled round the jagged rocks -- he could picture the place as he knew it , only ten times more pitiless and forbidding in the visionless darkness ; the wind soughed through the gorge with fearsome sighs , and rustlings and whisperings , and the bushes and grasses that grew in the ledges of the cliffs seemed to him like living creatures that danced and beckoned , shadowy and indistinct .
3 An owl laughed ` Hoo !
4 hoo ! '
5 almost in his face , as he peered over the edge of the gulf , and the old man threw himself back in a perspiration of horror .
6 He was afraid !
7 He drew back shuddering , and covering his face in his hands he wept aloud .
8 Presently he was aware of a gentle radiance that shed itself before him .
9 Surely morning was not already coming to hasten and reveal his disgrace !
10 He took his hands from before his face , and saw before him two lovely beings whom his instinct told him were not mortal , but were Peris from Paradise .
11 ` Why do you weep , old man ? '
12 said one , in a voice as clear and musical as that of the bulbul . '
13 I weep for shame , ' replied he .
14 ` What do you here ? '
15 questioned the other . '
16 I came here to die , ' said Wali Dad .
17 And as they questioned him , he confessed all his story .
18 Then the first stepped forward and laid a hand upon his shoulder , and Wali Dad began to feel that something strange -- what , he did not know -- was happening to him .
19 His old cotton rags of clothes were changed to beautiful linen and embroidered cloth ; on his hard , bare feet were warm , soft shoes , and on his head a great jewelled turban .
20 Round his neck there lay a heavy golden chain , and the little old bent sickle , which he cut grass with , and which hung in his waistband , had turned into a gorgeous scimetar , whose ivory hilt gleamed in the pale light like snow in moonlight .
21 As he stood wondering , like a XXXXX in a dream , the other peri waved her hand and bade him turn and see ; and , lo !	man		bushes|cliffs|creatures|grass|gulf|ledges|man|owl|rocks|shadows

16 His old cotton rags of clothes were changed to beautiful linen and embroidered cloth ; on his hard , bare feet were warm , soft shoes , and on his head a great jewelled turban .

1 ` Why do you weep , old man ? '
2 said one , in a voice as clear and musical as that of the bulbul . '
3 I weep for shame , ' replied he .
4 ` What do you here ? '
5 questioned the other . '
6 I came here to die , ' said Wali Dad .
7 And as they questioned him , he confessed all his story .
8 Then the first stepped forward and laid a hand upon his shoulder , and Wali Dad began to feel that something strange -- what , he did not know -- was happening to him .
9 His old cotton rags of clothes were changed to beautiful linen and embroidered cloth ; on his hard , bare feet were warm , soft shoes , and on his head a great jewelled turban .
10 Round his neck there lay a heavy golden chain , and the little old bent sickle , which he cut grass with , and which hung in his waistband , had turned into a gorgeous scimetar , whose ivory hilt gleamed in the pale light like snow in moonlight .
11 As he stood wondering , like a man in a dream , the other peri waved her hand and bade him turn and see ; and , lo !
12 before him a noble gateway stood open .
13 And up an avenue of giant place trees the peris led him , dumb with amazement .
14 At the end of the avenue , on the very spot where his hut had stood , a gorgeous palace appeared , ablaze with myriads of lights .
15 Its great porticoes and verandahs were occupied by hurrying servants , and guards paced to and fro and saluted him respectfully as he drew near , along mossy walks and through sweeping grassy lawns where fountains were playing and flowers scented the air .
16 Wali Dad stood stunned and helpless .
17 ` Fear not , ' said one of the peris ; ` go to your house , and learn that God rewards the simple-hearted . '
18 With these words they both disappeared and left him .
19 He walked on , thinking still that he must be dreaming .
20 Very soon he retired to rest in a splendid room , far grander than anything he had ever dreamed of .
21 When morning dawned he woke , and found that the XXXXX , and himself , and his servants were all real , and that he was not dreaming after all !	palace		Round|anything|chain|fountains|guards|hand|palace|something|voice|weep

1 I came here to die , ' said Wali Dad .
2 And as they questioned him , he confessed all his story .
3 Then the first stepped forward and laid a hand upon his shoulder , and Wali Dad began to feel that something strange -- what , he did not know -- was happening to him .
4 His old cotton rags of clothes were changed to beautiful linen and embroidered cloth ; on his hard , bare feet were warm , soft shoes , and on his head a great jewelled turban .
5 Round his neck there lay a heavy golden chain , and the little old bent sickle , which he cut grass with , and which hung in his waistband , had turned into a gorgeous scimetar , whose ivory hilt gleamed in the pale light like snow in moonlight .
6 As he stood wondering , like a man in a dream , the other peri waved her hand and bade him turn and see ; and , lo !
7 before him a noble gateway stood open .
8 And up an avenue of giant place trees the peris led him , dumb with amazement .
9 At the end of the avenue , on the very spot where his hut had stood , a gorgeous palace appeared , ablaze with myriads of lights .
10 Its great porticoes and verandahs were occupied by hurrying servants , and guards paced to and fro and saluted him respectfully as he drew near , along mossy walks and through sweeping grassy lawns where fountains were playing and flowers scented the air .
11 Wali Dad stood stunned and helpless .
12 ` Fear not , ' said one of the peris ; ` go to your house , and learn that God rewards the simple-hearted . '
13 With these words they both disappeared and left him .
14 He walked on , thinking still that he must be dreaming .
15 Very soon he retired to rest in a splendid room , far grander than anything he had ever dreamed of .
16 When morning dawned he woke , and found that the palace , and himself , and his servants were all real , and that he was not dreaming after all !
17 If he was dumbfounded , the merchant , who was ushered into his presence soon after sunrise , was much more so .
18 He told Wali Dad that he had not slept all night , and by the first streak of daylight had started to seek out his friend .
19 And what a search he had had !
20 A great stretch of wild jungle country had , in the night , been changed into parks and gardens ; and if it had not been for some of Wali Dad 's new servants , who found him and brought him to the palace , he would have fled away under the impression that his trouble had sent him crazy , and that all he saw was only imagination .
21 Then Wali Dad told the XXXXX all that had happened .	merchant		God|avenue|dream|flowers|hand|merchant|neck|room|shoulder|words

20 Every evening the king and his nobles were served on golden plates and from golden cups ; and the smaller people on silver plates and from silver cups ; and each evening each guest was requested to keep the places and cups that they had used as a remembrance of the occasion .

19 Besides the great dinners , there were sports and hunting , and dances , and amusements of all sorts .
20 On the fourth day the king of Khaistan took his host aside , and asked him whether it was true , as he had suspected , that he wished to marry his daughter .
21 But Wali Dad , after thanking him very much for the compliment , said that he had never dreamed of so great an honour , and that he was far too old and ugly for so fair a lady ; but he begged the XXXXX to stay with him until he could send for the Prince of Nekabad , who was a most excellent , brave , and honourable young man , and would surely be delighted to try to win the hand of the beautiful princess .	king		God|advice|air|daughter|day|end|hunting|invitation|king|streak

1 And up an avenue of giant place trees the peris led him , dumb with amazement .
2 At the end of the avenue , on the very spot where his hut had stood , a gorgeous palace appeared , ablaze with myriads of lights .
3 Its great porticoes and verandahs were occupied by hurrying servants , and guards paced to and fro and saluted him respectfully as he drew near , along mossy walks and through sweeping grassy lawns where fountains were playing and flowers scented the air .
4 Wali Dad stood stunned and helpless .
5 ` Fear not , ' said one of the peris ; ` go to your house , and learn that God rewards the simple-hearted . '
6 With these words they both disappeared and left him .
7 He walked on , thinking still that he must be dreaming .
8 Very soon he retired to rest in a splendid room , far grander than anything he had ever dreamed of .
9 When morning dawned he woke , and found that the palace , and himself , and his servants were all real , and that he was not dreaming after all !
10 If he was dumbfounded , the merchant , who was ushered into his presence soon after sunrise , was much more so .
11 He told Wali Dad that he had not slept all night , and by the first streak of daylight had started to seek out his friend .
12 And what a search he had had !
13 A great stretch of wild jungle country had , in the night , been changed into parks and gardens ; and if it had not been for some of Wali Dad 's new servants , who found him and brought him to the palace , he would have fled away under the impression that his trouble had sent him crazy , and that all he saw was only imagination .
14 Then Wali Dad told the merchant all that had happened .
15 By his advice he sent an invitation to the king and princess of Khaistan to come and be his guests , together with all their retinue and servants , down to the very humblest in the camp .
16 For three nights and days a great feast was held in honour of the royal guests .
17 Every evening the king and his nobles were served on golden plates and from golden cups ; and the smaller people on silver plates and from silver cups ; and each evening each guest was requested to keep the places and cups that they had used as a remembrance of the occasion .
18 Never had anything so splendid been seen .
19 Besides the great dinners , there were sports and hunting , and dances , and amusements of all sorts .
20 On the fourth day the king of Khaistan took his host aside , and asked him whether it was true , as he had suspected , that he wished to marry his daughter .
21 But Wali Dad , after thanking him very much for the compliment , said that he had never dreamed of so great an honour , and that he was far too old and ugly for so fair a lady ; but he begged the king to stay with him until he could send for the Prince of Nekabad , who was a most excellent , brave , and honourable young man , and would surely be delighted to try to win the hand of the beautiful XXXXX .	princess		advice|avenue|cups|daughter|flowers|palace|plates|princess|streak|words

1 ` Fear not , ' said one of the peris ; ` go to your house , and learn that God rewards the simple-hearted . '
2 With these words they both disappeared and left him .
3 He walked on , thinking still that he must be dreaming .
4 Very soon he retired to rest in a splendid room , far grander than anything he had ever dreamed of .
5 When morning dawned he woke , and found that the palace , and himself , and his servants were all real , and that he was not dreaming after all !
6 If he was dumbfounded , the merchant , who was ushered into his presence soon after sunrise , was much more so .
7 He told Wali Dad that he had not slept all night , and by the first streak of daylight had started to seek out his friend .
8 And what a search he had had !
9 A great stretch of wild jungle country had , in the night , been changed into parks and gardens ; and if it had not been for some of Wali Dad 's new servants , who found him and brought him to the palace , he would have fled away under the impression that his trouble had sent him crazy , and that all he saw was only imagination .
10 Then Wali Dad told the merchant all that had happened .
11 By his advice he sent an invitation to the king and princess of Khaistan to come and be his guests , together with all their retinue and servants , down to the very humblest in the camp .
12 For three nights and days a great feast was held in honour of the royal guests .
13 Every evening the king and his nobles were served on golden plates and from golden cups ; and the smaller people on silver plates and from silver cups ; and each evening each guest was requested to keep the places and cups that they had used as a remembrance of the occasion .
14 Never had anything so splendid been seen .
15 Besides the great dinners , there were sports and hunting , and dances , and amusements of all sorts .
16 On the fourth day the king of Khaistan took his host aside , and asked him whether it was true , as he had suspected , that he wished to marry his daughter .
17 But Wali Dad , after thanking him very much for the compliment , said that he had never dreamed of so great an honour , and that he was far too old and ugly for so fair a lady ; but he begged the king to stay with him until he could send for the Prince of Nekabad , who was a most excellent , brave , and honourable young man , and would surely be delighted to try to win the hand of the beautiful princess .
18 To this the king agreed , and Wali Dad sent the merchant to Nekabad , with a number of attendants , and with such handsome presents that the prince came at once , fell head over ears in love with the princess , and married her at Wali Dad 's palace amidst a fresh outburst of rejoicings .
19 And now the King of Khaistan and the Prince and Princess of Nekabad , each went back to their own country ; and Wali Dad lived to a good old age , befriending all who were in trouble and preserving , in his prosperity , the simple-hearted and generous nature that he had when he was only Wali Dad Gunjay , the grass cutter .

6 On the fourth day the king of Khaistan took his host aside , and asked him whether it was true , as he had suspected , that he wished to marry his daughter .
7 But Wali Dad , after thanking him very much for the compliment , said that he had never dreamed of so great an honour , and that he was far too old and ugly for so fair a lady ; but he begged the king to stay with him until he could send for the Prince of Nekabad , who was a most excellent , brave , and honourable young man , and would surely be delighted to try to win the hand of the beautiful princess .
8 To this the king agreed , and Wali Dad sent the merchant to Nekabad , with a number of attendants , and with such handsome presents that the prince came at once , fell head over ears in love with the princess , and married her at Wali Dad 's palace amidst a fresh outburst of rejoicings .
9 And now the King of Khaistan and the Prince and Princess of Nekabad , each went back to their own country ; and Wali Dad lived to a good old age , befriending all who were in trouble and preserving , in his prosperity , the simple-hearted and generous nature that he had when he was only Wali Dad Gunjay , the grass cutter .

11 Tale of a Tortoise and of a Mischievous Monkey Once upon a time there was a country where the rivers were larger , and the forests deeper , than anywhere else .
12 Hardly any men came there , and the wild creatures had it all to themselves , and used to play all sorts of strange games with each other .
13 The great trees , chained one to the other by thick flowering plants with bright scarlet or yellow blossoms , were famous hiding-places for the monkeys , who could wait unseen , till a puma or an elephant passed by , and then jump on their backs and go for a ride , swinging themselves up by the creepers when they had had enough .
14 Near the rivers huge tortoises were to be found , and though to our eyes a tortoise seems a dull , slow thing , it is wonderful to think how clever they were , and how often they outwitted many of their livelier friends .
15 There was one tortoise in particular that always managed to get the better of everybody , and many were the tales told in the forest of his great deeds .
16 They began when he was quite young , and tired of staying at home with his father and mother .
17 He left them one day , and walked off in search of adventures .
18 In a wide open space surrounded by trees he met with an elephant , who was having his supper before taking his evening bath in the river which ran close by .
19 ` Let us see which of us two is strongest , ' said the young tortoise , marching up to the elephant .
20 ` Very well , ' replied the elephant , much amused at the impertinence of the little creature ; ` when would you like the trial to be ? '
21 ` In an hour 's XXXXX ; I have some business to do first , ' answered the tortoise .	time		amusements|camp|country|elephant|merchant|mother|nature|preserving|princess|time

1 By his advice he sent an invitation to the king and princess of Khaistan to come and be his guests , together with all their retinue and servants , down to the very humblest in the camp .
2 For three nights and days a great feast was held in honour of the royal guests .
3 Every evening the king and his nobles were served on golden plates and from golden cups ; and the smaller people on silver plates and from silver cups ; and each evening each guest was requested to keep the places and cups that they had used as a remembrance of the occasion .
4 Never had anything so splendid been seen .
5 Besides the great dinners , there were sports and hunting , and dances , and amusements of all sorts .
6 On the fourth day the king of Khaistan took his host aside , and asked him whether it was true , as he had suspected , that he wished to marry his daughter .
7 But Wali Dad , after thanking him very much for the compliment , said that he had never dreamed of so great an honour , and that he was far too old and ugly for so fair a lady ; but he begged the king to stay with him until he could send for the Prince of Nekabad , who was a most excellent , brave , and honourable young man , and would surely be delighted to try to win the hand of the beautiful princess .
8 To this the king agreed , and Wali Dad sent the merchant to Nekabad , with a number of attendants , and with such handsome presents that the prince came at once , fell head over ears in love with the princess , and married her at Wali Dad 's palace amidst a fresh outburst of rejoicings .
9 And now the King of Khaistan and the Prince and Princess of Nekabad , each went back to their own country ; and Wali Dad lived to a good old age , befriending all who were in trouble and preserving , in his prosperity , the simple-hearted and generous nature that he had when he was only Wali Dad Gunjay , the grass cutter .

18 So he wound it carefully round the elephant 's leg , and tied it in a firm knot .
19 ` Now ! '
20 cried he , plunging into a thick bush behind him .
21 The whale tugged at one end , and the XXXXX tugged at the other , and neither had any idea that he had not the tortoise for his foe .	elephant		bones|bush|creature|elephant|fountain|leg|legs|mouth|signal|water

1 ` Let us see which of us two is strongest , ' said the young tortoise , marching up to the elephant .
2 ` Very well , ' replied the elephant , much amused at the impertinence of the little creature ; ` when would you like the trial to be ? '
3 ` In an hour 's time ; I have some business to do first , ' answered the tortoise .
4 And he hastened away as fast as his short legs would carry him .
5 In a pool of the river a whale was resting , blowing water into the air and making a lovely fountain .
6 The tortoise , however , was too young and too busy to admire such things , and he called to the whale to stop , as he wanted to speak to him .
7 ` Would you like to try which of us is the stronger ? '
8 said he .
9 The whale looked at him , sent up another fountain , and answered : ` Oh , yes ; certainly .
10 When do you wish to begin ?
11 I am quite ready . '
12 ` Then give me one of your longest bones , and I will fasten it to my leg .
13 When I give the signal , you must pull , and we will see which can pull the hardest . '
14 ` Very good , ' replied the whale ; and he took out one of his bones and passed it to the tortoise .
15 The tortoise picked up the end of the bone in his mouth and went back to the elephant . '
16 I will fasten this to your leg , ' said he , ` in the same way as it is fastened to mine , and we must both pull as hard as we can .
17 We shall soon see which is the stronger . '
18 So he wound it carefully round the elephant 's leg , and tied it in a firm knot .
19 ` Now ! '
20 cried he , plunging into a thick bush behind him .
